## **Diet and Lifestyle in the Postpartum Period** 

Your postpartum diet and lifestyle are vital for you and your baby. Adopting a healthy diet and lifestyle will help you achieve better physical and mental health during this critical period. It will also help you lose the excess weight you have gained throughout pregnancy which decreases your risk of obesity, type II diabetes and cardiovascular diseases[[1]] . 

Adopting a healthy diet and lifestyle postpartum will also help you provide optimal care for your baby and support adequate breastfeeding. This, in turn, provides your baby with the best nutrition and all the essential nutrients they need[[2]] . This chapter provides information on the appropriate strategies to maintain a healthy diet and lifestyle after childbirth. 

**==> picture [185 x 185] intentionally omitted <==**

## **02 Weight Loss Recommendations for Breastfeeding and Non-Breastfeeding Mothers** 

It is natural to feel eager to lose your pregnancy weight after childbirth. Remember that immediately after delivery, you will lose approximately 6 kg (including your baby’s weight, ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ��������������������������������������������������������������������[ [3]] . If you are breastfeeding ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� because some of the fat stored in your body during pregnancy and the energy from the food you eat, will be utilized for breastmilk production. 

����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� loss goals depend on whether you are overweight or obese or have gained a lot of weight during pregnancy. You need to consult with a dietitian to develop your weight loss plan. In any case, extreme energy restriction and quick weight loss should be avoided. Box 1 provides some general weight loss recommendations, whether you are breastfeeding your baby or not[[3-5]] . 

## Box 1. Weight loss recommendations for breastfeeding and non-breastfeeding mothers 

Following a healthy diet and exercising will help you lose up to 0.5 kg/week: 

- However, it may take up to 6 months to a year to return to your pre-pregnancy weight, regardless of whether you are breastfeeding or not. 

- If you are breastfeeding, it is not advisable that you lose weight quickly. So do not lose more than 2 kg/month. 

Making the right food and beverage choices is critical in promoting healthy weight loss: 

- ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Choose low-fat milk and dairy products 

- ����������������������������������������������������������� 

- Limit your intake of sugar, salt, and unhealthy fats 

- Limit your consumption of high sugar foods (sweets, desserts, candies, confectioneries, ����������������� 

- Limit your consumption of fried foods and fast food 

- Control portion sizes 

Be active and incorporate exercise into your weekly lifestyle. Refer to section 4.

## **2. Dietary choices:** 

In addition to having an adequate energy intake that supports breastfeeding, you should also focus on making healthy choices and following a healthy, diverse, and nutritious diet that includes the six food groups. For more information on how to follow a healthy varied diet, please see section 1.4 in chapter 1.  Box 3 below presents a few recommendations for breastfeeding mothers. 

## Box 3. Dietary recommendations for breastfeeding mothers 

- Diversify your diet and consume foods from the six food groups listed in Appendix 1. 

- Choose foods rich in nutrients (vegetables, fruits, whole grain cereals, low-fat milk and dairy ��������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Ensure adequate intake of calcium-rich foods such as low-fat milk, dairy products, calcium-enriched products, and green leafy vegetables. Section 2b provides you with more examples of calcium-rich foods. 

- ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� presented in section 2c.

- ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ All of these are high in saturated fat. 

- The consumption of products that contain partially hydrogenated oils (such as fast foods, fried foods, ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������

## **Do Not:** 

- DO NOT adopt strict dietary restrictions such as omitting certain food groups; instead, follow a healthy, diverse, and balanced diet.

## **2b. Calcium** 

In addition to vitamin D, healthy bones and teeth require an adequate level of calcium consumption. Calcium is essential for many functions in the body. Therefore, it is critical that you consume an adequate amount of calcium daily (1000 mg per day)[[11]] . You can achieve this amount of calcium intake by including rich sources of calcium in your diet. The richest natural sources of calcium is milk followed by tofu, as shown in Table 3. 

Table 3. What is equivalent to the calcium in 1 cup of milk (300 mg calcium)? 

- 1 cup yogurt (low-fat or skimmed) 

- 50 grams of white low-salt cheese 

Calcium-rich alternatives are shown in Table 4[ [12-16]] . If you have a low dietary intake of calcium, the doctor may prescribe you calcium supplements. 

Table 4. Calcium content of selected non-dairy foods

|Tofu (only those processed with calcium<br>sulfate- check label)|1 cup|600|

|Sardines|½ cup|300|
|Salmon, canned with bones|½ cup|220|

## **��� ��������������������������������������������������������������** 

In the local culture and many other cultures, the use of herbal supplements to increase milk production has been passed down from one generation to the other. It is important to clarify that herbal supplements contain numerous physiologically active compounds, and it is likely that ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������[[32]] . ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� but the safety of its usage is still unknown[[33]] . To increase your breastmilk production, it is ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� by the baby.

## **��� �������������������������������������������������** 

Breastmilk is the best nutrition for your baby. If you consume or are exposed to harmful substances, these can pass to your breastmilk and your baby. This section discusses the following harmful substances: smoking, energy drinks, and mercury. 

- **Smoking:** �������������������������������������������������������������������������� transmitted to your baby through your breastmilk. Nicotine can increase the risks of sudden infant death syndrome and respiratory allergies and can lead to poor growth and ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� your baby. It can also reduce the amount of vitamin C the baby receives through your breastmilk[[34]] .  For your health and the health of your baby, abstain from smoking, and ensure a smoke-free environment at home. 

- **Energy Drinks:** ������������������������������������������������������������������������ �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� can cause dehydration, insomnia, as well as increased blood pressure and heart rate[[35]] . Consequently, this may cause health problems for you and your baby[ [24-26]] . A breastfeeding woman should not consume energy drinks. 

- **Mercury:** ������������������������������������������������������������������������� breastfeeding, as they may contain chemical contaminants, particularly mercury[ [23]] . 

Fish is an important source of high-quality protein and omega-3 fatty acids (which are a healthy type of fat for you ��������������������������������������������������������� ������������������������������������������������������� contaminants such as mercury. For this reason, limit the ������������������������������������������������������������� **���** tuna bigeye. Refer to component 2c in section 3.1 for more ���������������������������������������������� consume or to limit. 

## **��� ��������������������������������������������������������������������������** 

Some women may choose to follow a vegetarian or vegan diet. 

���������������������������������������������������������������� 

- **Vegetarian diet:** generally, characterized by excluding ��������������������������������������������������������� you can consume animal-based byproducts such as milk and dairy products, honey, and eggs. 

- **Vegan diet:** this is characterized by excluding all ������������������������������������������������ �������������������������������� 

**==> picture [124 x 118] intentionally omitted <==**

If you are following a vegan or vegetarian diet, you are still capable of producing nutritionally valuable milk for your baby, as long as your diet is balanced and you are meeting all your nutritional requirements[[36]] . Hence, if you are following a vegan or vegetarian diet, you need to pay particular attention to the following nutrients: vitamin B12, calcium, iron, zinc, iodine, and omega-3 fatty acids. These nutrients are crucial to support your health and well-being  as a mother and are vital for the baby’s optimal growth and development[ [27]] . In many instances, vegan diets need to be supported by some supplementation, such as vitamin B12. Make sure to plan your diet with a dietitian and discuss the issue of supplementation with the doctor. 

Box 5 below provides a few recommendations for vegan and vegetarian breastfeeding mothers [27, 37].

- Consume a variety of plant-based foods (beans, lentils, tofu, soy-based meat substitutes, unsalted, ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Combine legumes with whole grain cereals in your meals. This will improve the quality of the protein you are consuming. 

- ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� beverages contain substances that decrease iron and calcium absorption. 

- �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ����������������������������������������������������� 

- Use iodized table salt. Iodine is crucial for the baby’s brain and nervous system development. 

- Include choline-rich foods in your diet, such as beans, peas, and lentils. Choline is needed to support the baby’s spinal cord development.

## 3.2. For mothers who are not breastfeeding: nutritional needs 

If you are not breastfeeding, your nutritional needs are similar to those of other adult women in ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� pregnancy while following a balanced, varied, and adequate diet as well as an active lifestyle. 

The recommended rate of weight loss is 0.5 kg per week. For this purpose, you can decrease your energy intake from food by 500 kcal per day or increase your physical activity to burn an additional 500 kcal per day. Consult with a dietitian for a well-planned diet and meal plan. 

## 3.3. For mothers who had gestational diabetes 

If you developed gestational diabetes during pregnancy, you need to know that your blood glucose levels will return to normal shortly after delivery. It is, however, important to reevaluate your glucose levels at 4 - 12 weeks after delivery. If your results are normal, then screening for prediabetes and diabetes should be repeated every 1 - 3 years, depending on your overall medical status[ [44]] . 

Keep in mind that the fact that you developed gestational diabetes will put you at a higher risk of having type 2 diabetes later in life[ [45]] . This is why it is very important that you: 

- Adhere to a balanced and adequate diet (rich in vegetables, fruits, whole grain cereals, and ��������� 

- ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Restrict your intake of added sugar, sugar-sweetened beverages, sugar-containing foods, and desserts. 

- Maintain an active lifestyle. 

**==> picture [166 x 151] intentionally omitted <==**

## **04 Physical Activity Recommendations for Postpartum Mothers** 

It is advisable that you resume or engage in low-intensity physical activity as soon as possible level, promote some weight loss, and reduce the risk of postpartum depression and anxiety[[46]] . 

You need to discuss the appropriate timeframe for your return to pre-pregnancy exercise levels with your healthcare practitioner. In most cases, mothers can gradually resume moderate to vigorous physical activity 4-8 weeks after natural delivery and 8-12 weeks after a cesarean section[[47]] . 

Box 7 below provides some recommendations on physical activity based on the Qatar National Physical Activity Guidelines[[47]] . 

- Box 7. Recommendations on physical activity for women after delivery • Do not stay in bed all day. Consider taking a walk in the neighborhood or in a park. • Postpartum, focus on low-intensity exercises and gradually increase the frequency to reach 5 days/ week before increasing to moderate-intensity exercise. 

- • Incorporate moderate-intensity physical activity into your daily routine in coordination with your healthcare practitioner: o For previously active women - 150 minutes/week, 3 - 5 days/week; start with 3 days a week, then gradually increase to 5 days a week. 

- o For previously inactive women - 45 minutes/week, increasing it to 150 minutes a week. 

- • Engage in muscle and bone-strengthening exercises 2 - 3 days a week. • It is recommended that you use moderate weights and aim for 8 - 12 repetitions* and 1 - 2 sets per exercise (for cesarean delivery, no abdominal workout for a minimum of 4 months).

- • You may want to include your baby in your exercise routine, especially if you are short on time. For example, consider taking your baby for a walk in the stroller. 

- • Examples of exercise options include walking, cycling, water aerobics (or aqua gym), swimming, Pilates, and light weight training.

## **Your Sleep in the Postpartum Months** 

�������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� newborns experience several months of exhaustion from inadequate sleep. Indeed, your baby may wake up several times during the night for feeding, diaper change or simply because they seek comfort[ [53]] . 

The below recommendations can help you in getting more sleep: 

- Rearrange your sleep schedule: Many newborns have unpredictable sleep schedules. This is why it is recommended that you try to sleep whenever your baby sleeps, both at night and during day naps. This can help you get adequate sleep each day/night. Remember that your newborn may sleep up to 17 hours a day, which is much more sleep than you need[ [54]] . 

- • Share the night workload if you can: If feasible for you, you can alternate with your husband or a family member the responsibilities of taking care of your baby at night[[55]] . 

- Communicate with the doctor: You should communicate with the doctor if you are having problems sleeping, even if you think they are just a normal part of being a new mother[ [55]] . 

- • Know your baby’s sleep patterns: The phase when your baby wakes up several times in the night will not last forever. As your baby gets older, he/she will start to sleep for longer periods.

**==> picture [173 x 173] intentionally omitted <==**

## **Your Mental Health in the Postpartum Period** 

## 6.1. Postpartum depression 

The postpartum period is a time of transition for you and your family when you need to make important physical, psychological, and social adjustments. During this period, you may be at risk of developing anxiety[[56]] . Postpartum anxiety may occur because of hormonal changes that take place in this period. It may also occur as a response to real stressors, such as worrying about your baby’s health, learning to take care of him/her, or assuming new roles in your relationships[[57]] . It is normal to feel a bit down and anxious in the postpartum period; this usually lasts for a maximum of 2 weeks after giving birth and is known as “baby blues”. 

��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������[[58]] , which is usually experienced by one out of ten women[ [58-60]] . PPD may occur anytime between birth and one year after childbirth. It is a severe mood disorder that can remove the joy and happiness of  a new mother[ [61]] . 

If you think you have PPD, anxiety or any other mood disorder, it is important to consult the ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� and your relationship with your child[[61]] ���������������������������������������������������� �������������������������������������������������������������������������[ [62]] ��������������������� your willingness and capacity to breastfeed[[62]] . 

Be aware of the early signs and symptoms of PPD, which include the following: 

1. Sadness and crying episodes 

2. Lack of enjoyment 

3. Loss of interest 

4. Feeling overwhelmed 

5. Feelings of anger and fear 

6. Mood swings 

7. Agitation 

8. Lack of appetite or overeating 

9. ��������������������������������������������� 

10. ������������������������������ 

11. Problems concentrating 

**==> picture [187 x 187] intentionally omitted <==**

Recommendations for preventing and addressing anxiety and postpartum depression are presented in Box 10[ [47, 58, 63, 64]] . 

## Box 10. Recommendations for preventing and addressing anxiety and postpartum depression 

- Rest well, whenever possible, and get enough hours of sleep every night since lack of sleep has been linked with mood changes. 

- Increase your physical activity: Exercise can help boost your mood and reduce episodes of postpartum depression. 

- Those exercises could be something as simple as pushing your baby stroller and getting some fresh air. 

- Cuddle your baby: this releases a hormone which can lower anxiety levels. 

- Talk to your husband, family, and friends about your feelings; this can help alleviate the symptoms. 

- Connect with other new mothers to share experiences. 

- Find more self-time to do more of the things you enjoy, such as reading a book or taking a warm shower. 

- Ask for help such as with the household chores. 

- Follow a healthy diet regularly and do not go for prolonged hours without eating. Refer to section 3 in this chapter. 

- Seek professional support if you are having postpartum depression, since the depression ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� baby and family. 

## 6.2. Fathers’ support during the postpartum period 

Fatherhood can be a milestone in a man’s life that comes with excitement and challenges. The �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� undergo this transition smoothly. Box 11 presents tips on how fathers can be engaged during the postpartum period. 

## Box 11. Tips for fathers on how they can be engaged during the postpartum period 

- Encourage your wife and assure her that all will be okay. 

- Allow your wife to talk freely about her feelings and emotions without being judgmental. 

**==> picture [102 x 111] intentionally omitted <==**

- Provide emotional and practical support and guidance when she needs it. 

- Encourage your wife to seek professional support and treatment if she is struggling. 

In addition to their supportive role to the mother and family, fathers need to be wary of their own mental state. Fathers may also experience postnatal depression, particularly if the mother is depressed. Symptoms can include anxiety, low mood, irritability or anger, changes in appetite ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� children. Fathers should therefore seek professional help if needed. 

## 6.3. Family support in the postpartum period 

You and your husband may need additional help to ease your adjustment to the new baby. A family member such as the grandmother or aunt may provide much needed support in the few days and weeks after childbirth. 

Whoever you decide to have as support, make sure to have clear and transparent communications about what you expect them to help with. Communication is vital in preventing misunderstandings ����������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

## 6.4. Eating disorders during the postpartum period 

Given that the postpartum period may be associated with heightened levels of anxiety and stress, women during this vulnerable period of their lives may experience some forms of eating disorders such as emotional eating or binge eating (consuming large amounts of food in a small ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� disorders including bulimia nervosa (eating large amounts of food followed by self-induced ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������[[65]] . Hence, �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� disorder specialist and/or mental health professional if you think your attitudes towards food and/or eating have changed or your attitude or feelings about your own body, weight or shape have changed.

## **Importance of Medical Follow-Up in the Post-Partum Period** 

Since the weeks following birth represent a critical period in a woman’s life, you may want to schedule a follow-up appointment with your physician in the few weeks after the delivery[ [66]] . It is also important to seek medical care if you feel unwell or have developed any medical condition post-partum. If you have a diagnosed health condition such as hypertension, cardiovascular disease, type II diabetes, or any other chronic illness, it is important that you schedule regular ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� to your health condition, close medical attention, and a proper lifestyle will help minimize the risk of complications on your health.

## **Practical tips:** 

- Choose a wide variety and color of vegetables. 

- Eat vegetables with most meals, including snacks. 

- Favor fresh vegetables in season. 

- Choose vegetables prepared with little or no added salt, oil or fat. 

- Eat adequate amounts daily since vegetables help you feel full and are low in calories. 

- �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� nutrients.

- In restaurants, order salad dressing separately and limit how much you add. 

- Eat raw vegetables as snacks with low-fat dip.

- ������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- When eating out, choose plain vegetables instead of breaded or deep fried. 

- Avoid adding butter or margarine and rich sauces to vegetables. Use fresh or dried spices, herbs, or lemon juice instead. 

- Favor fresh vegetables over canned ones. When choosing canned vegetables, make sure to rinse them with water to lower the salt content. 

- Add a plate of fresh cut vegetables with your main meals.

- Fruit salad makes a healthy dessert.

- Favor fresh or unsweetened frozen fruits instead of canned fruits packed in syrup. 

- ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� milk alternative such as soy beverage with a combination of fresh or frozen fruit. 

- Fresh fruit pieces on a stick make a great snack.

## **Practical tips:** 

- ���������������������������������������������������������������������������

- Try vegetarian dishes from other cultures such as bean burritos and dahl. 

**==> picture [74 x 118] intentionally omitted <==**

- Rinse canned beans with water before using to decrease salt content.

- Cooking dry legumes instead of canned varieties is cheaper and decreases packaging waste, and exposure to chemicals from cans.

## **Poultry and meat: Choose skinless poultry and lean cuts of meat:** 

- Remove skin from poultry before cooking or buy skinless pieces. 

- Limit your consumption of red meat to no more than 500 g per week[[14]] . 

- Limit your consumption of processed luncheon and deli meats such as corned beef, hot dogs, sausages, ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� Make sandwiches with lower fat, unprocessed options such as roast beef, turkey, or chicken. 

- ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� and beef tenderloin. 

- Tenderize lean cuts of meat by using a marinade or a slow cooking method such as stewing or braising. 

- ���������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Broil, grill, roast, poach or boil poultry and meat instead of frying. 

- If frying meat, use little oil. 

- ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ 

- Limit pre-prepared breaded foods such as nuggets. 

## **Eggs:** 

- Limit raw or partially cooked eggs or foods containing raw eggs. 

- Boiled eggs are nutritious and great to have for breakfast or dinner. 

## **Nuts and seeds:** 

- Aim for unsalted, raw nuts and seeds, rather than fried ones. 

- ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Top your salad with unsalted, raw nuts or seeds. 

- Add some unsalted, raw nuts to your yogurt or oatmeal in the morning. 

“For more information on alternatives for animal-based protein foods, see Box 8. Dietary recommendations for vegetarian and vegan women.”

## **01** 

**==> picture [213 x 42] intentionally omitted <==**

**“Take Care of Your Family”** is the 8[th] key recommendation from the Qatar Dietary Guidelines, and within this recommendation there is an extensive emphasis on the importance [1]. Breastfeeding is in fact the ideal source of nutrition for your baby. It promotes adequate child’s growth, development and health[[2, 3]] , protects the child against infectious diseases (including ear infection, diarrhea, and pneumonia) and decreases the risk of  newborn mortality. Studies have also shown that breastfeeding may decrease your child’s risk of developing obesity, type 2 diabetes and other chronic diseases later in life[ [4]] . 

The WHO and UNICEF recommend early initiation of breastfeeding (i.e., within one hour after birth), exclusive breastfeeding for six months, followed by continued breastfeeding for up to two years of age and beyond[ [3, 5]] . Every woman is encouraged to provide the greatest and safest care for her baby through breastfeeding, without the use of infant formulas, unless medically required. The [3, 6, 7]  . 

## **For the mother** 

- It improves mother-infant bonding 

- It burns calories and promotes weight loss 

- It helps decrease postpartum bleeding 

- It helps the uterus return to its normal size 

- It reduces the risk of ovarian and breast cancer 

- It helps in pregnancy spacing (a natural but not the ideal method for birth control) 

- It reduces the risk of developing postpartum depression 

- It lowers the risk of type 2 diabetes 

**==> picture [123 x 122] intentionally omitted <==**

## **For the baby** 

- It reduces newborn mortality 

- It strengthens the child’s immune system 

- It reduces the risk of overweight/obesity later in life 

- It decreases the risk of developing chronic diseases such as type 2 diabetes, later in life 

- It reduces the risk of developing allergies and asthma 

- It reduces the risk of developing digestive system problems 

- It is associated with better mental health later in life

## **03 Breastfeeding Recommendations and Practical Tips** 

## 3.1.    Breastfeeding recommendations 

The WHO and UNICEF recommendations regarding breastfeeding are summarized below: 

Early initiation of breastfeeding 

Exclusive breastfeeding for of life 

Continued breastfeeding for up to two years of age and beyond 

This means that you should put **the baby to the breast birth.** This practice helps in decreasing the baby’s risk of infections and mortality after birth. It will also make it easier for you to exclusively breastfeed later on. 

It is recommended that you exclusively breastfeed your baby from 0 to six months of age[[3, 5]] . 

Mothers are encouraged to **continue breastfeeding their child for up to two years of age and beyond,** while making sure to introduce safe and nutritious complementary foods at six months of age. Refer to Chapter 5 for details about complementary feeding. 

This means that **NO other foods or liquids can be provided to your baby** (not even water or herbal drinks). months of life while your baby is exclusively breastfed, he/ she does not require any extra water. Human milk provides all 

Medications, oral rehydration solution (for the treatment of diarrhea), or vitamins and minerals supplements do not count as foods or liquids but can be given to the baby if prescribed by the doctor.

**==> picture [117 x 118] intentionally omitted <==**

At around six months of age, your baby will need to consume healthy and nutritious foods, in addition to breastmilk, to meet his/her energy and nutrient needs. Therefore, at six months, solid foods, such as vegetables, fruits, infant cereals, and mashed meat, should be introduced to complement your child’s diet while maintaining breastfeeding for up to two years and beyond[ [1]] . 

����������������������������������������������������������������������� 1000 days of life, and is a critical period for your child’s growth and development for his/her future health. It is also the period when malnutrition may start to develop in many children[ [2]] , which can have ������������������������������������������������������������������ can lead to delayed physical and mental development, decreased intellectual performance, and poor health later on during adolescence and adulthood. Once a child reaches two ���������������������������������������������� �����������������������������������[[3]] . 

“The transition from exclusive breastfeeding to family foods, referred to as complementary feeding, typically covers the period from 6 to 18 - 24 months of age.” 

-World Health Organization (WHO)[[4]        ] 

**==> picture [266 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
01 Assessment of the  Child’s Growth<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


It is important that the doctor routinely assesses the growth of your child. Growth is, in fact, an important indicator of the child’s overall health, development, and nutritional status. the doctor ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� age of your child[[5-7]] . 

In some instances, these measurements may reveal certain growth-related problems, such as: 

- The child has a low weight for his/her age (underweight or severely underweight): This may result from the child being too thin or too short for his/her age. It may also be the result of poor nutrition or certain illnesses. Underweight children are more vulnerable to diseases and may experience delays in their development. 

- The child is short for his/her age (stunted or severely stunted): If your child’s height is below the normal range for his/her age (a condition referred to as stunting), this may be the result of long-term malnutrition or illness. It has consequences on the child’s physical development and is often accompanied by poor mental development. 

- The child has lost weight (wasted or severely wasted): This may occur because of poor nutrition or illness. Wasted children have a higher risk of contracting diseases and illnesses. 

- The child is overweight or obese: Infants and young children can become overweight or obese ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� genetic factors (i.e., running in the family), or a combination of all these factors. In rare cases, obesity in children may result from a medical condition diagnosed by the doctor. Being overweight and obese early in life can increase the risk of obesity and chronic diseases later in life. 

- The child has a larger-than-normal or smaller-than-normal head circumference: This helps in assessing brain size as an indicator of possible developmental or nutritional problems. 

**==> picture [104 x 106] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [140 x 114] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [206 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
02 Continued Breastfeeding<br>(for Up to Two Years and Beyond)<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


For optimal growth and development, the World Health Organization (WHO) and United Nations International Children’s Emergency Fund (UNICEF) recommend continued breastfeeding for up to two years and beyond while introducing adequate complementary foods (solid and soft foods) at six months of age. 

����������������������������������������������������������������������[ [8-10]] 

- Breastmilk provides your baby with important nutrients (but make sure to introduce solid foods in addition to breastmilk at six months of age). 

- Breastmilk strengthens your child’s immunity; as long as you breastfeed your baby, the antibodies and cells in your breastmilk will continue to enhance your baby’s immune system. 

In addition, breastfeeding reduces your risk of certain illnesses, such as the risk of developing ovarian cancer, breast cancer, high blood pressure, heart disease, type 2 diabetes and rheumatoid arthritis. 

**==> picture [157 x 158] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [36 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
03<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **Dietary Recommendations** 

Adequate child growth depends on several factors, including appropriate energy and nutrient intake. Starting complementary feeding is a must at six months since milk alone will not meet all ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ of nutrient-dense foods from the following general food groups: 

- Breastmilk (or infant formula milk in certain conditions) 

- Vegetables: 

   - Vitamin A rich vegetables such as pumpkin, carrots, sweet red peppers, squash, and dark green leafy vegetables. 

   - Other vegetables such as broccoli, zucchini and cucumbers. Try to choose vegetables ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� vitamins and minerals. 

- Fruits: 

   - Vitamin A rich fruits such as ripe mangoes and ripe papayas. 

   - ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ 

- �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� and potatoes. 

- Legumes: such as beans and lentils. 

- Dairy products: such as yogurt, labneh and cheese. 

- �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� eggs. 

Section 6 provides information on the texture (consistency) ���������������������������������������������������������� 

Section 12 provides details on what foods to avoid to prevent choking and tips on how to prepare these foods.

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
04<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


**What are the Signs to Start the Complementary Feeding (Weaning) Process?** 

������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ complementary feeding[[11]] : 

- Your baby can stay in a sitting position with their head held steady with no support 

- Your baby has full coordination between their eyes, hands, and mouth by locating the food and attempting to pick it up to place in their mouth. 

- Your baby can properly swallow their food and not spit it out 

- Your baby seems hungry earlier than usual 

- Your baby shows interest in food when others are eating 

- Your baby can let you know if they do not want to eat by turning their head away from the food or leaning back 

- Your baby’s weight has doubled compared to their birth weight. 

**==> picture [157 x 156] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [36 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
05<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **How to Start the Complementary Feeding (Weaning) Process?** 

As previously mentioned, complementary feeding should begin at six months of age; however, the weaning process could take days, weeks, or months depending on your baby’s growth and development[[12]] . You can begin by slowly decreasing how often or how long you are breastfeeding and/or formula feeding each day.  Begin with substituting one breastfeed with complementary feed, and once your baby is accustomed to this, you can substitute another feed, and so on. Remember that the weaning process should be gradual, and the pace is up to you and your baby[[12]] . 

**==> picture [177 x 177] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [225 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
06 Food Consistency (Texture)<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


It is recommended to gradually thicken food consistency as the baby ages, while paying attention to your baby’s abilities. Infants can usually eat puréed, mashed and soft/semi-solid ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� (i.e., foods and snacks that the baby can grasp and eat alone). 

By the age of 12 months, most children can consume the same types of foods as the rest of the family (while making sure that these are nutritious foods)[[4, 13]] . Refer to section 12 to learn more about the risk of choking and how to avoid it, and to section 14 for examples of nutritious foods and tips on preparing foods for your baby.

## **07 Introduction of Soft, Semi-Solid and Solid Foods** 

## **�������������������������������������������������** 

���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� to keep them plain (i.e., no added salt, sugar, or strong spices). 

��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� However, many experts agree that you should start with foods that contain iron, since it is needed for many areas of the baby’s development[[11]] ������������������������������������������ �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� (such as rice and oatmeal), vegetables, and fruits are also good sources of iron and other vital minerals and vitamins. 

It is very important that you introduce one single-ingredient food to your baby at a time. For example, introduce peaches alone rather than peach-yogurt, which has more than one ingredient. Introducing a single new food at a time, at 3-5 days intervals, will allow you to  identify any adverse reaction, allergic response or intolerances that your baby may have to certain foods[ [11]] . 

## **6-8 months** 

At around six months of age, you can introduce strained or puréed foods to your baby’s diet (see �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� your baby directly from a squeezable pouch. 

**==> picture [117 x 144] intentionally omitted <==**

Some dietitians recommend introducing vegetables before fruits. This is because the sweetness of fruits will make vegetables less ������������������������������� ����� 

“Your baby needs more energy and nutrients now than at any other time in their life.” 

-UNICEF[[14]                          ] 

����������������������������������������������������� 

## **�����������������������������������������������������[[11]] :** 

- Well-cooked mashed vegetables, puréed meat, well-cooked mashed legumes (e.g., beans, lentils, peas (peeled), rice, mashed potatoes, and whole-fat dairy products (such as yogurt) 

- Soft, diced, or mashed fruits such as bananas , apples (puréed after boiling), ripe mangos, avocados, and melons 

- �������������������������� 

- All foods should be natural without the addition of salt, sugar, or spices 

Introduce a single new food at a time, at 3-5 day-intervals. This approach will allow you to identify any adverse reaction, allergic response or intolerances that your baby may have to ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

Examples   of nutritious, suitable, and easy to prepare meals for your baby (6-8 months old): 

- Puréed: potatoes + carrots + peas, mashed with fork (without salt) 

- Puréed: potatoes + carrots + peas, mashed with fork + boiled chicken 

- Puréed: potatoes + carrots + peas, mashed with fork + part of boiled egg 

- Pumped breastmilk or formula milk + mashed boiled rice (without salt) 

- Mashed lentil soup: rice + mashed lentils + potatoes + carrots 

- Mashed: boiled rice + spinach, mulukhiya (jute leaves), beans, or basil (without salt) 

- Puréed: Apples (boiled, yellow, or green, or red), ripe 

- Mashed bananas and avocado 

If you have to use ready-made foods, read the nutrition label and buy high-quality, natural baby foods that do not contain unhealthy fats, added sugar, salt,  or chemical preservatives[[15]] . 

**==> picture [154 x 153] intentionally omitted <==**

���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� add enough breast milk or formula to the infant cereal (do not use cow’s milk) for a relatively thin consistency so that it is easy for your baby to swallow. Just 2 feedings a day, a couple of spoonfuls, is all your baby needs[[11]] . 

When introducing store-bought infant cereals, check the food label to ensure that the cereal is a single-ingredient product and does not contain other added ingredients such as cow’s milk, ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� reactions to the cereals (See section 17 on food allergies and section 14 on tips related to preparing foods for your baby). 

## **How much food should I give my baby?** 

- Complementary feeding should be introduced at six months, however most of your baby’s daily calories will still come from breastmilk (or formula) at this age. Once you start introducing complementary feeding, your baby may only be eating one meal per day along with breastmilk (or formula). This is completely normal; start slowly and progressively increase the number of meals daily. 

- Eventually, you can give 3 meals per day to your 6-8 months old baby, with an amount close to 2-3 tablespoons of food per meal[ [16]] , while maintaining breastfeeding (or formula ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� his/her appetite. 

- Observe your child for signs of hunger or satiety (see section 8)[[16]] . 

- If you are formula feeding, do not exceed more than 950 mL (32 oz.) in 24 hours[[11]] . Overfeeding can cause vomiting, diarrhea, or excessive weight gain. 

An example of choices to include in a one-day menu is provided in Box 2[[17]] .

- ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� foods in small amounts. Your baby will show signs that they are ready to eat on their own by trying to grab the food or the spoon with their hands.

Your baby is learning, and he/she will soon be able to get the skills ���������������������������������������������������������������������[[18]] .

����������������������������������������������������������� 

- Allowing your child to feed themselves is the best technique to allow them to experience their senses such as taste, texture and smell. 

- Nurturing independence (giving them a little control over when they take a bite, how much they put in their mouth, and when they are done eating). 

- Enhancing grasping and coordination skills. 

- Allowing them to be aware of their bodies (knowing where their mouth is, how to bend ������������������������������������������������� 

- Helping in oral motor development (how wide they need to open their mouth, close their ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

## **12-24 months:** 

- ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ per day at 12-24 months of age, with additional nutritious snacks (such as a piece of fruit), 1-2 times per day[[16]] . 

- Per meal, your child can consume an amount close to ¾  cup, i.e. around 190 mL[[16]] . Appendix 1 provides information on the recommended number of servings from each food group, and examples of servings.

08 Hunger and Satiety Cues<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Feed your child at his or her own comfort and observe signs of hunger and satiety (feeling of fullness). Below are hunger and satiety cues that your child may display (Box 4)[[19]] . 

## Box 4. Hunger and satiety cues that your child may display 

## **Hunger cues** 

## **Satiety cues** 

- Moves head towards spoon/food, bottle, or nipple 

- Smiles during feeding to indicate they want more 

- Points out or reaches for food 

- Gets excited when he/she sees food 

- Expresses with words or sounds the ������������������������� 

- Turns head away from spoon/food, bottle, or nipple 

- Spits food out of their mouth 

- Shakes head to say no more 

- Clenches mouth, closes and refuses to open 

- Plays with the food, spoon, or plate

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
09<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **How to Boost My Child’s Immunity** 

Children need adequate intakes of nutrients and vitamins to help boost their immune system and protect them against infectious germs. However, there are no magical foods or supplements that will boost your child’s immune system[ [20]] . The best advice for supporting your child’s immunity is ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� a varied and balanced diet consisting of food and beverages from the six food groups.  Prioritize nutrient-rich foods such as vegetables and fruits that supply an abundant amount of antioxidants, vitamins, and minerals. Make sure to limit their consumption of processed foods, snacks, and foods high in sugar, salt, and unhealthy fats. Consult Appendix 1 for details pertinent to dietary recommendations for your child. 

**==> picture [323 x 213] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [132 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
10 Picky Eating<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Some children go through a phase of refusing certain types of food, especially when trying �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� occurs among young children and is often perceived as a stressful and frustrating experience for parents. Picky eating rarely causes serious nutritional, medical or growth problems for the child, and it is a phase that most children grow out of[[21]] ����������������������������������������� habits, and they can be considered as “extreme picky eaters.” Professionals may consider this as a feeding disorder, and you will need the help of a health professional to help your child grow out of �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������[[21]] . 

## **Picky eaters & extreme picky eaters** 

Extreme picky eating can last for a longer time as compared to picky eating. Extreme picky eaters can grow out of it over months or years; however, this usually needs to be done with the help of a health professional[ [21]] . Extreme picky eating is usually more common in children with social communication disorders[[21]] . 

Although being a picky eater is usually harmless, being an extreme picky eater can cause serious �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������[ [21]] . Table 1 provides you with a direct comparison between the two behaviors. 

**==> picture [158 x 156] intentionally omitted <==**

Table 1. Comparison between picky eating and extreme picky eating characteristics in children ���������������������� 

||**Picky eating**|**Extreme picky eating**|
|---|---|---|

## **When to seek professional help?** 

Do not get frustrated if your child is a picky eater. Just provide your child with healthy food choices and try your best to help your child grow out of this phase. Most picky eaters remain healthy, and their eating habits tend to gradually improve as they age[ [22]] . If your child’s picky ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������[ [21]] .

   - Do not use desserts as a reward for eating other foods; this approach will make desserts more exciting for them and, as a result, may impact their acceptance of other foods. 

   - Encourage good behavior at mealtime by rewarding the child with “non-food” items. 

   - • Verbal praise or a loving look are considered positive in developing food preferences. 

- Decide what ������������� • ������������������������������������������������������������ child ���������������������������������������������� • ����������������������������������������������������������� if possible. But make sure that it is suitable for the child’s age (such as mashed, chopped, etc.). 

   - Try adding herbs and spices to make the food tastier for the child. 

   - �������������������������������������������������������������� ��������������������������������������������������� 

   - ������������������������������������������������������ again. 

   - �������������������������������������������������������������� and appropriate to their needs. 

   - ����������������������������������������������������������� ������������������������������������������������������� preference. 

   - • Try to mix the new food item with a food the child likes. For example, adding mashed peas to mashed potatoes. 

   - • When the child accepts new food items, use these items as ������������������������������������������������������������� color, and texture. For example, if your child likes mashed ���������������������������������������������������������� or carrots. 

   - • Encourage children to feed themselves as much as possible �������������������������������������������������������������� them to acquire self-feeding skills and thus become more independent. 

- Stick to your plan • Many children go through a picky eating phase which usually causes no harm. Try not to overthink it and do your best to help them grow out of it. 

- • ����������������������������������������������������������� accepts it. Young children are naturally cautious when trying �������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Do NOT • Do NOT count calories or weigh your child daily. • Do NOT label your child as “the picky eater.” • Do NOT lower expectations by assuming or telling your child “You won’t like anything.” 

- • Do NOT complain about your child’s eating behavior in front of them. 

- • Do NOT inspect your child’s lunchbox or ask about their food intake the moment you see them. 

- • Do NOT punish or scold your child for not eating. 

**==> picture [139 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
11 Force-Feeding<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Parents tend to get worried when their child eats very little, refuses to eat a meal or refuses to eat healthy foods like fruits and vegetables[[24]] ������������������������������������������������� especially if the child is underweight or not gaining weight adequately. For others, unconsumed meals can be a source of frustration, especially when the parents do not like to waste food that has been prepared, or when they believe that children have to ‘clean their plates’[[24]] . As a result, parents may force their child to eat their entire meal. 

The child may indeed eat a bit more when forced to eat. However, studies have shown that the act of forcing the child to eat can cause long-term problems, which are summarized below: 

- **Disliking or avoiding certain foods.** This can result from a negative experience of being ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� and unpleasant experiences that may accompany them[ [24]] . 

- **Disturbing the child’s ability to respond to their internal feelings of hunger and fullness.** This means that the child would not listen to their body, so food intake becomes determined by factors other than what the body requires. Instead, the child learns to continue eating, even when they feel full, stopping only when the plate is empty[[24]] . 

- **Development of overeating and overweight.** As explained above, forcing the child to eat can disturb the child’s ability to learn appropriate appetite control. This can eventually lead to overeating and the development of overweight and obesity[[24]] . 

Except in rare cases, children are usually extremely good at knowing when they are full and ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ they will eat when feeling hungry. It is also important to remember that children naturally tend ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� and accept that children may refuse to eat it, as this is a normal part of their development. Also remember that the stomachs of young children are smaller than adults’ and you may be serving too much food that your child cannot consume.

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
12 Choking Hazards in Young Children<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## **What are the choking hazards for 6-12 months old babies?** 

Young children are at a risk of food-related choking. Even if they started developing teeth, this �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� vegetables, whole cherry tomatoes, raw apple slices, raw pear slices, whole kernels of corn, and whole olives. (Note: carrots and corn, can be cooked and then diced). 

- Slippery foods: including whole small fruits, such as grapes, berries and cherries, large pieces of meat, poultry and cheese, hot dogs, and hard candy such as lollipops, jellybeans, and cough drops. (Note: meat, poultry and other protein foods should be well-cooked and then cut lengthwise or chopped up into smaller pieces (less than 0.5 cm in size). Grapes should be cut into quarters.) 

- Sticky foods:������������������������������������������������������������������������� jellybeans, dried fruits, and fruit leathers. (Note: smooth nut butters should be spread in a thin layer on a food, such as bread; they should not be given straight from a spoon or a ������� 

- Fruits with seeds or stones: whole fruits with stones inside such as cherries, plum, etc. 

- Foods that can form crumbs in your baby’s mouth include bread, hard cookies/biscuits. 

**==> picture [106 x 108] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [126 x 126] intentionally omitted <==**

**Tips to keep your baby safe from choking** 

- �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� can mash them with their tongue on the top of their mouth). 

- Cut potentially hazardous foods into smaller bite-sized pieces. 

- Supervise your child during all meal and snack times. 

- Do not let other children feed your child unless they are supervised closely. 

- Encourage your child to chew their food well before swallowing 

- Your baby should always be sitting upright when eating, never leaning backwards, or laying down. 

- Your child should not be walking or running while eating 

- Let your baby eat at their own pace and let them put the food into their mouth. 

- Vacuum and sweep regularly to remove choking hazards in the home. 

- Remove any unsafe food or toys from your child’s reach. 

## **What are choking hazards for children 12 to 24 months old?** 

Most of your baby’s teeth would have already come in at this age, making chewing and swallowing a lot easier. By now, your child is able to eat most foods, however there are still some choking hazards. How food is prepared, cooked, and served can still impact the incidence of choking. You should therefore still be cutting or mashing certain foods, to prevent choking. Below are examples of foods that are potential choking hazards for your babies between the ages of 12 and 24 months[ [25]] .

## **13 Are There Any Foods My Baby Should Not Eat?** 

- Cow’s milk: ���������������������������������������������������������������������������� infants cannot digest cow’s milk. 

- Honey: Honey should never be given to babies under the age of one year, as there is a risk of infant botulism (food poisoning). 

- Raw or runny eggs:����������������������������������������������������������������� year of life. Runny eggs can contain bacteria such as salmonella that cause food poisoning. ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� poached, boiled, or fried). 

- Camel milk:������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Goat milk: ����������������������������������������������������������������������������� ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� given to your baby if recommended by the doctor. 

- Sugary drinks or sweet foods: ������������������������������������������������������������ candies, carbonated soda, or energy drinks. 

- Salt: Try to avoid puréeing adult foods and feeding them to your child as they may contain large amounts of salt. Your baby’s kidneys are not yet fully developed to cope with large amounts of salt, and the longer you wait to introduce it into their diet, the better chance you have of not encouraging a liking for it. 

- �������������� Culturally speaking, the older generations have the misconception that if ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� overstimulate your baby and may also prevent the baby’s body from absorbing iron from their food. 

**==> picture [156 x 129] intentionally omitted <==**

- Low-fat foods (especially dairy products): Low-calorie cheeses, yogurts or spreads should not be given to babies. Always feed your baby the full-fat varieties as their body needs it. 

- Foods that can lead to choking: Foods that may stick to the roof of the mouth and foods ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ section 12 for more information on the risk of choking). 

Beyond six months of age, there is no reason to delay the introduction of common food allergens ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� regularly and monitor if any signs of an allergic reaction occur. If you have concerns, please speak to the doctor.

14<br>How to Prepare Foods for Your Baby<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Recommendation 8 of the Qatar Dietary Guidelines (QDG), which is “Take care of your family,” heavily focuses on the following: 

“Build and model healthy patterns for your family” 

- Keep regular hours for meals. 

- Eat at least one meal together with your family every day. 

- Be a role model for your children when it comes to healthy eating and physical activity. 

��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� recommends for healthy adults, the same recommendations can be given here; always limit sugar, salt and unhealthy fats/oils when preparing your baby’s meals and adopt healthier cooking methods such as boiling, steaming, baking and grilling. Do not add sugar, salt, and spices to the child’s food before 12 months of age. 

The following section presents examples of nutritious foods to introduce to your baby’s diet as well as the cooking/preparation method that will best preserve the foods’ nutritive value. The information is presented per food group.

## **FRUITS** 

Feeding your baby fresh fruit is quite easy as it requires no cooking and requires very little preparation time. 

|**Strawber-**<br>**ries**||These delicious and nutrient-packed fruits may cause<br>an allergic reaction to some babies, so be careful when<br>introducing them into your baby’s diet. |---|---|---|



They say an apple a day, keeps the doctor away. With regards to babies, apples may not protect them from all illnesses they may be exposed to, but they sure can boost immunity as they are packed with vitamin C and can help prevent episodes of constipation which can be quite uncomfortable and painful for your little one. At six months of age, your toddler may not be able to chew apples properly, therefore the best solution is to purée your apples. **Apple** • Cut and peel apples then bring them to a boil in a saucepan. Simmer for about 10 minutes or until the apples are very soft. Allow apples to cool and blend them well (no need to add any ingredients to the purée). • Once your baby has developed teeth and is able to chew properly, you can serve them raw apple slices as a delicious snack. These delicious tropical fruits can be added to your baby’s diet right at six months as they are an excellent source of ��������������������������������������������������������� sweet treat is better eaten ripe, as they require little prep time. • Just mash the mangoes with a fork and they can be eaten on their own or mixed with mashed veggies or other ingredients. **Mangoes** • Try adding mashed or puréed mangoes with any of the following foods: oatmeal, yogurt, avocado, carrot, sweet potato, banana, or apple. • Puréed mangoes can also be frozen for up to three months, so freeze them in small batches and only thaw �������������������������������������������������� eaten on their own.

## **CEREALS & STARCHY VEGETABLES** 

Cereals and starchy vegetables provide your baby with a major source of energy (calories) and represent an essential part of a healthy diet to ensure your child’s normal growth and development. Cereals and starchy vegetables are excellent sources of complex ������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

The initial foods introduced within this food group are infant cereals and starchy vegetables such as potatoes and peas. You can mix the infant cereal with your breastmilk, water, or formula (if you were already formula feeding your infant). Make sure the cereal is of thin consistency to allow for easy swallowing. As your baby gets older and develops more motor skills, you can increase the thickness.

## **LEGUMES** 

Legumes represent an important food group, and their consumption should be encouraged daily as ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� and zinc, which makes them a good substitute for these animal foods. They are also an excellent ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� Legumes include but are not limited to chickpeas (hummus and nakhi), and fava beans (bajela and foul mudammes). 

## **MILK, DAIRY PRODUCTS & ALTERNATIVES** 

Human milk (or infant formula in certain situations) should be the only type of milk given to infants and young children. Cow’s milk as well as goat and camel milk should be avoided until 12 months of age, as young infants cannot digest it. The high concentration of protein and minerals in those milk types can stress your baby’s kidneys and cause anemia, fever, or diarrhea. You should also limit giving these ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� to your baby if recommended by the medical practitioner). 

�������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� cannot introduce other dairy products in your child’s diet. Hard cheeses, cottage cheese and yogurt can be given to your baby as a protein source but ensure to observe them closely for any reaction after introducing these foods.

## **FISH, POULTRY, MEAT & ALTERNATIVES** 

Fish, poultry, and meat are excellent sources of protein and essential minerals and should be introduced into your baby’s diet after six months of age. As your baby reaches six months of age, iron stores begin to decrease which is why iron and zinc found in protein-rich foods such as meat become crucial. 

Iron and zinc are essential nutrients to combat the ��������������������������������������������������������� �������������������������������������������������� **Iron and** developmental behavior. Therefore, proper complementary **Zinc** feeding should include foods rich in iron and zinc to help support the production of red blood cells, to build a strong immunity and foster normal brain development. 

- �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� food before introducing a new one, to make sure that your baby did not develop any reaction to these ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- ��������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� �������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Broil, boil, roast or bake these foods and limit frying and deep frying. 

- Thoroughly cook these foods to kill any bacteria. 

- Once cooked, cut up these foods and purée them to the desired consistency. 

- Use a separate blender for your baby’s foods, or make sure the blender is thoroughly cleaned before using it to prepare your baby’s foods. 

- As your baby’s feeding skills mature, you no longer need to purée these foods, you can start ��������������������������� 

- ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� them).

## **EGGS** 

Eggs are an excellent source of protein and are safe to introduce to your baby after six months of age. However, you must ensure they are cooked thoroughly. Although, egg allergies are common, it is not a reason to fear introducing this excellent source of protein into your baby’s diet. Just take special care to observe for any rashes or allergic reactions after its introduction. 

Raw or partially cooked eggs or foods that contain eggs, such as mayonnaise, icing, homemade ice cream, salad dressings etc., should NEVER be given to infants as they contain bacteria that may cause severe illness in your baby. 

## **BEVERAGES** 

**Water** 

**Fruit Juice** 

**==> picture [84 x 371] intentionally omitted <==**

Once you begin introducing complementary foods at six months of age, you can start giving your baby small amounts of water. Considering that the climate in Qatar can become quite hot, it would be a good idea to check with a doctor on how much water you should be giving your baby. Encourage your child to drink from a cup rather than a bottle, as this will promote the development of your child’s motor skills. This can start as early as six months of age. 

Unless otherwise advised by the doctor, infants less than 12 months of age should not be given juices since these may replace other, more nutritious foods. After your child is one year old or older, you can start giving them small amounts of fresh homemade juice or fresh 100% pasteurized fruit juice ���������������������������������������������������������� a bottle. 

It is important to note that giving fruits (puréed or diced) to your baby instead of juices is always better since fruits are ��������������������������������������������������������������� ��������������������������������������������������������� reduce their appetite from eating nutritious whole foods. Giving your baby too much juice may also cause a diaper rash, diarrhea or unwanted weight gain because juices contain a lot of calories as compared to fresh fruits[[29-31]] . 

Are unpasteurized fruit juices safe for consumption? When ������������������������������������������������������ ������������������������������������������������������������ ������������������������������������������������������������� ������������������������������������������������������� because it comes in more playful packaging. It is important, however, to ensure that the juice clearly states it is pasteurized, as unpasteurized juices may carry the bacteria E. coli which can make your baby very ill[ [32, 33]] .

15 Oral Health<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


Feeding practices in infancy may increase the risk of early childhood caries, which are also known as baby bottle caries. Babies derive comfort from sucking on the bottle, and this may help them relax and fall asleep. However, you should not give a bottle of formula, juice or any other sweetened beverages to calm your baby or at bedtime since this will lead to teeth demineralization and, thus, tooth decay[[34, 35]] . 

The American Academy of Pediatric Dentistry and the American Academy of Pediatrics have provided recommendations to prevent early childhood caries;[[30, 35]] : 

- Limit frequent consumption of liquids and solid foods that contain sugar. In particular, avoid ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ and sweetened milk to your baby. 

- ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� 

- Your baby should be weaned from the bottle at 12-18 months of age. 

- At the age of 12 months, you should be taking your baby to see a dentist. Talk to the ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� emerges. 

- Use a gentle brush or a clean, soft cloth to wipe emerging baby teeth and promote good �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� right before bed.

**16 Supplements** 

Always consult with the doctor before giving your baby any supplements. In most instances, a balanced and diverse diet will meet your child’s needs for the various nutrients. Thus, once your ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� the 6 food groups in order to meet their nutritional needs.

The American Academy of Pediatrics recommends that all breastfed infants (whether exclusively or partially breastfed), and ����������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� take vitamin D supplements. When your baby starts complementary feeding, you can add the vitamin D drops to puréed food or porridge[[36]] . 

- **Iron:** ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������ ������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� them meet iron requirements. Speak to the doctor about iron supplements and how long you should continue iron supplementation after your baby starts eating iron-containing �������������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� supplement[[36]] . 

- **Vitamin B12:** �������������������������������������������������������������������������� meat, eggs and dairy. Most children do not have any problem with this nutrient. However, for vegan moms who are breastfeeding their baby and who plan to introduce only plantbased foods into their child’s diet, there will be a need for vitamin B12 supplements. ���������������������������������������������������������������������������������������� formula, which is most often soy-based[[36]] . 

- **Other vitamins:** ������������������������������������������������������������������������ day should not be given any other vitamin supplements. This is because infant formula is ���������������������������[ [37]] .
